الرسل
حجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that allowing prophets to suffer harm contradicts divine wisdom.
Correction: The passage shows that apparent harm is outweighed by eternal compensation, so it is actually wise.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why did God not exterminate the disbelievers immediately without sending messengers?
A) Because He was unable to do so.
B) Because He wanted to establish proof and avoid being accused of injustice.
C) Because He wanted to give the messengers a chance to suffer.
D) Because He wanted to test the messengers.

Key Takeaway
The apparent folly of prophets' suffering is actually profound wisdom when seen in light of eternal compensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face harm or persecution for your faith, do you truly believe that God's compensation is so great that you would wish for more harm? Examine your heart: are you willing to endure temporary suffering for eternal bliss, or do you seek immediate comfort?
?
The passage says that if God punished without sending messengers, people would call Him unjust. Are you quick to accuse God of injustice when you see believers suffer, or do you trust His hidden wisdom? What specific trial in your life have you questioned God's wisdom about?
?
If you were a prophet and knew the eternal reward, would you actually desire more harm as the passage suggests? Why do you find it hard to let go of worldly safety? Commit today to one act of courage for the sake of truth, trusting in the compensation you cannot yet see.

Sending Prophets: Possible and Required by Divine Wisdom
Al-Nasafi states that sending prophets is possible and, for Maturidi scholars, obligatory in terms of divine wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi scholars affirm that sending prophets is both possible and, as a requirement of divine wisdom, obligatory in a specific sense.

Core Idea
Divine wisdom makes prophethood a necessity, not just a possibility.

In this passage, al-Nasafi lays out the rational grounding for prophethood. He first establishes that sending messengers is logically possible — there is no impossibility in it. Then, he goes further: for the verifying scholars of the Maturidi school, it is not merely possible but obligatory, not because God is compelled by any external force, but because His own wisdom demands it. This distinction is crucial for avoiding the Mu'tazilite view of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice the structure: it moves from possibility (imkan) to obligation (wujub) via the requirement of wisdom.
The passage cuts off, but the main argument is complete up to that point.
2
Identify the two categories
Find 'mumkinat' (possibilities) and 'wajibat' (obligations). Understand what each means in the context of divine actions.
Obligation here is not legal obligation but a requirement of wisdom.
3
Understand the key term 'muqtada al-hikma'
The phrase 'min muqtadayat hikmat al-hakim' means 'from the requirements of the wisdom of the Wise.' This is the basis for the Maturidi claim of obligation.
4
Reflect on the distinction from Mu'tazila
Consider how this view differs from the Mu'tazila, who held that God is under a legal obligation to send prophets. The commentary clarifies this.
The passage hints at an earlier explanation ('ala nahw ma bayyantu).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ثبت أن إرسال الرسل من الله تعالى إلى عباده في حيز الممكنات وأنه لا امتناع له. إرسال الرسل من مقتضيات حكمه الحكيم. ثم عند المحققين من متكلمي أصحابنا هو في حيز الواجبات أعني أنه من مقتضيات حكمة الحكيم جل وعلا على نحو ما بينت، ثم على قول هؤلاء وإن كان
And if it is established that sending messengers from God—exalted is He—to His servants is in the realm of possibilities (imkanat) and that there is no impossibility for it. Sending messengers is from the requirements of His wise judgment. Then, according to the verifiers among the theologians of our companions, it is in the realm of obligations (wajibat), meaning that it is from the requirements of the wisdom of the Wise—majestic and exalted is He—in the manner that I have explained. Then, according to the statement of these, although it...
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage establishes two points about sending prophets. First, it affirms that sending messengers is possible (mumkin) from God, with no inherent impossibility. Then it elevates the status: according to the verifying scholars (muhaqqiqun) of the Maturidi school, sending prophets falls into the category of obligations (wajibat) in a specific sense: it is a requirement of God's wisdom (hikmah). They mean that because God is wise, it is necessary that He send messengers to guide His servants. However, the text is cut off, so we do not get the full explanation of how this differs from the Mu'tazila view of obligation. The author indicates he has explained it earlier ('ala nahw ma bayyantu).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Maturidis and Mu'tazila agree that God is obligated to send prophets in the same sense.
Correction: Maturidis hold that sending prophets is a requirement of divine wisdom, not a duty imposed on God by an external standard. The Mu'tazila view imposes a legal-like obligation on God.

Checkpoint

A) Impossible
B) Possible but not necessary
C) In the realm of obligations as a requirement of divine wisdom

Key Takeaway
For Maturidi scholars, sending prophets is not just possible but necessitated by God's wisdom, without implying any external compulsion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If rejecting the prophets is rejecting God’s wisdom, how seriously do you take the Sunnah in your daily decisions? Pinpoint one habit where you have been negligent and commit to reviving it this week.
?
The passage says sending messengers is a requirement of God’s wise judgment. Does your heart truly see prophethood as a necessary gift, or do you sometimes feel it is an extra burden? How can you increase your gratitude for being guided by a prophet today?

Maturidi Position on Sending Messengers and Refutation of Mu'tazila and Khawarij
Al-Nasafi refutes Mu'tazila's wujub based on al-aslah and establishes the Maturidi view on proof for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi view on the sending of messengers as both possible in itself and necessary from divine wisdom, and why a claimant must bring a proof.

Core Idea
Messengers are sent by divine wisdom, but each claimant must prove his claim.

Al-Nasafi addresses a key debate in Islamic theology: is sending messengers obligatory upon God? The Mu'tazila say yes, based on what is most beneficial (al-aslah). The Maturidis affirm that sending messengers is possible and, from divine wisdom, necessary, but they reject the Mu'tazili grounding. This passage also takes on a radical sect that accepts prophethood without any proof. Let's see how al-Nasafi navigates these issues.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Maturidi stance on rational good and evil
Notice that al-Nasafi begins by aligning al-Maturidi with those who affirm rational discernment of good and evil. This sets the stage for the discussion.
This is important because it grounds the possibility of arguing ethically.
2
Examine the Mu'tazili position and its refutation
Al-Nasafi states the Mu'tazili view that sending messengers is wajib because it is aslah, but calls it 'corrupt speech' and promises a later refutation.
He does not fully refute here but warns his companions.
3
Grasp the Maturidi distinction: possible but necessitated by wisdom
Al-Nasafi says that sending messengers is mumkin (possible) with no impossibility, but for the verified scholars it is in the realm of obligations due to divine wisdom.
This is a nuanced position: necessary in one sense, possible in another.
4
Understand the need for a proof for each claimant
Al-Nasafi argues that even though sending messengers is necessary in divine wisdom, the designation of a specific individual is a possibility requiring proof.
This refutes those who would accept any claim without evidence.
5
Learn the refutation of the Ibadiyya/Khawarij
Al-Nasafi rejects the Ibadiyya claim that the mere statement of a prophet must be accepted without proof, because any contingent thing needs a proof.
This shows the Maturidi insistence on rational evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على مثل هذه الطريقة يتكلم الشيخ أبو منصور الماتريدي، وكل من يقول من أصحابنا بإمكان استدراك الحسن والقبيح بالعقل. فأما المعتزلة فإنهم يقولون بوجوب إرسال الرسل، لما أن ذلك أصلح للعباد وما هو الأصلح للعباد فهو واجب التحصيل على الله تعالى وذلك كلام فاسد نبين فساده عند بلوغنا إلى إبطال القول بالأصلح، فينبغي لمن يتكلم من أصحابنا في هذه المسألة أن يكون بصيرًا بذلك ليصون نفسه في إثبات الرسالة عن الوقوع في القول بالأصلح، والله الموفق. وإذا ثبت أن إرسال الرسل من الله تعالى إلى عباده في حيز الممكنات وأنه لا امتناع له. ثم عند المحققين من متكلمي أصحابنا هو في حيز الواجبات أعني أنه من مقتضيات حكمة الحكيم جل وعلا على نحو ما بينت، ثم على قول هؤلاء وإن كان هو في نفسه واجب الوجود على ما قررنا، فهو في حق كل فرد من أفراد الناس زمان جواز ورود الرسول وتعين هذا المدعي له في حيز الممكنات أيضا، إذ الدليل عند القائلين بوجوبه إن دل على ثبوته لا محالة، فلا دليل يدل على ثبوته في حق هذا الشخص المتعين، فلابد إذا لكل مدَّع يدعي ذلك في حق نفسه من إقامة دليل يدل على تعينه لذلك، لأن تعينه له من جملة الممكنات فلا يثبت إلا بدليل يوجب ثبوته. وبهذا يعرف بطلان قول الإباضية من الخوارج أن مجرد قول الرسول واجب القبول وأن تعرى عن الدليل، وأن الراد عليه يكفر في ساعته. إذ لا ثبوت لما جاز عليه العدم إلا بدليل يوجب ثبوته.
In this manner speaks the Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, and everyone among our companions who affirms the possibility of discerning good and evil by reason. As for the Mu'tazila, they affirm the obligation of sending messengers, because that is the most beneficial (aslah) for the servants, and what is the most beneficial for the servants is obligatory to obtain on God—and that is corrupt speech, which we clarify corruption when we reach the refutation of the doctrine of al-aslah. So it is incumbent upon whoever among our companions speaks on this matter to be insightful so that he protects himself in establishing the messengership from falling into the doctrine of al-aslah. And God is the granter of success. And if it is established that sending messengers from God to His servants is in the realm of possibilities, and that there is no impossibility in it. Then according to the verified scholars among the theologians of our companions, it is in the realm of obligations—that is, it is a requirement of the wisdom of the Wise, Majestic and High, in the manner I have explained. Then, according to these scholars, even though it is in itself necessarily existent upon what we have established, it is with respect to every individual among people at the time of possibility of the messenger’s arrival and the designation of this claimant to it also in the realm of possibilities. For since the proof, according to those who affirm its obligation, indicates its existence necessarily, there is no proof indicating its existence with respect to this specific individual. Therefore, every claimant who claims that for himself must present a proof indicating his designation for it, because his designation is among possibilities, so it is only established by a proof that necessitates its existence. And by this is known the invalidity of the statement of the Ibadiyya from the Khawarij that the mere statement of the messenger is obligatory to accept, even if it is devoid of proof, and that one who rejects it is a disbeliever instantly. Because there is no existence for that which admits non-existence except by a proof that necessitates its existence.
Source pages p. 693

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi position on the sending of prophets (irsal al-rusul). It begins by aligning Shaykh Abu Mansur al-Maturidi with those who affirm the ability of the intellect to discern good and evil (al-husn wa al-qubh al-'aqli). Then it refutes the Mu'tazila, who argue that sending messengers is obligatory upon God (wajib 'ala Allah) because it is the most beneficial (aslah) for humanity, and whatever is the most beneficial must be done by God. Al-Nasafi calls this 'corrupt speech' (kalam fasid) and promises to refute it later when discussing the refutation of the doctrine of al-aslah. He warns his own companions to be cautious in arguing for prophethood to avoid falling into the Mu'tazili error. Next, he asserts that sending messengers is possible (mumkin) for God, with no impossibility. Then he clarifies that for the verified Maturidi theologians, it is in the realm of obligations (hizz al-wajibat) as a requirement of divine wisdom (hikma). However, even though in itself it is necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud) from the perspective of divine wisdom, it remains possible (mumkin) with respect to each individual person and the specific designation of that claimant to prophethood. Therefore, no individual can simply claim prophethood; they must present a proof (dalil) to show that they are specifically designated for it. This serves as a refutation of the Ibadiyya sect among the Khawarij, who claimed that the mere statement of a prophet must be accepted without proof, and that anyone who rejects it becomes a disbeliever instantly. The passage concludes by emphasizing that anything that is subject to non-existence (i.e., not necessary in itself) can only be established by a proof that necessitates its existence. The structure follows a logical order: (1) affirmation of rational good/evil, (2) refutation of Mu'tazili wujub based on al-aslah, (3) establishment of possibility, (4) clarification of divine wisdom making it necessary, (5) individual possibility requiring proof, (6) refutation of Ibadiyya who reject the need for proof.

Key Terms
إرسال الرسل
واجب
ممكن
أصلح
دليل
إباضية
خوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Maturidi view that sending messengers is necessary from divine wisdom is the same as the Mu'tazili view that it is obligatory upon God.
Correction: Al-Nasafi distinguishes: Mu'tazili wujub is based on al-aslah (most beneficial) as an obligation upon God, while Maturidi necessity is a requirement of divine wisdom, not an obligation upon God. Furthermore, the Maturidi necessity is general, and individual designation requires proof.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi, why is it necessary for a claimant to prophethood to present a proof even though sending messengers is necessarily existent in divine wisdom?
A) Because the claimant may be lying.
B) Because prophethood is always a punishment from God.
C) Because the designation of a specific individual is a possibility that requires proof.
D) Because the Mu'tazila require it.

Key Takeaway
Sending messengers is possible and required by divine wisdom, but every claimant must bring a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between the general necessity of sending messengers and the individual claimant needing proof. Have you ever accepted someone’s claim to be speaking for God (a scholar, leader, or even a personal inspiration) without demanding evidence from the Qur’an and Sunnah? What would it mean for you to apply this principle of requiring proof before accepting a religious authority?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God must do what is best for people (al-aslah), but al-Nasafi calls this corrupt speech. Do you carry any hidden expectation that God owes you something—comfort, success, or an easy path? How does the Maturidi view that God acts by wisdom, not obligation, challenge your assumptions about what you deserve from Him?
?
The Ibadiyya thought that rejecting a messenger’s statement without proof is instant disbelief. Have you ever been quick to judge someone who disagrees with you on a religious matter, assuming they are misguided or even outside the fold? How can this lesson’s insistence on careful proof before acceptance change the way you engage with differing views?

Definition and Source of the Miracle (Mu'jiza)
Al-Nasafi defines miracle, states its division into two types, and explains its basis in incapability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a prophetic miracle in Islamic theology: its division into two types, its basis in human incapacity, and its role as proof of prophethood.

Core Idea
A miracle is a proof of prophethood rooted in the challenger's incapacity.

Al-Nasafi begins a new section on the proofs of prophethood by defining the central concept: the miracle (mu'jiza). He establishes that the proof is called a miracle, divides it into two types, and then explains that its essential basis is the incapacity ('ajz) of the one challenged to produce something similar. This passage lays the groundwork for understanding how miracles function as divine confirmations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Al-Nasafi uses a heading to frame the topic.
The passage ends abruptly due to a lacuna in the manuscript.
2
Identify the definition of mu'jiza
Find where Al-Nasafi states that the proof (dalil) is called a miracle. Understand that 'mu'jiza' is a specific term for a prophetic proof.
3
Understand the division and the source
The passage mentions that the miracle divides into two types, but only the source (ma'khadh) is explained here. The source is 'ajz, incapacity.
The etymology connects the name to incapacitating the challenger.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why does Al-Nasafi emphasize the source in terms of incapacity? How does this relate to the challenge (tahaddi)?
This will be expanded in later passages.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إقامة الدلالة على ثبوت الرسالة: ثم الدليل يسمى معجزة وهو في نفسه ينقسم إلى قسمين فيحتاج إلى القول في مأخذها وحدها وبيان قسميها ووجه كيفية دلالتها على صدق الآتي بها. مأخذ المعجزة: فأما مأخذها فهو العجز الذي هو نقيض القدرة سميت معجزة لأنها تظهر عجز من يتحدى به عن معارضتها
Establishing the proof for the confirmation of messengership: Then the proof is called a miracle (mu'jiza), and it in itself divides into two divisions. So we need to speak about its source (ma'khadh) and its division alone, and the explanation of its two types, and the aspect of how it indicates the truthfulness of the one who brings it. [The source of the miracle:] As for its source, it is incapacity ('ajz), which is the opposite of power (qudra). It was called a miracle because it shows the incapacity of the one who is challenged by it to oppose it.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This passage defines the proof of prophethood known as mu'jiza (miracle). The author begins with a heading: 'Establishing the proof for the confirmation of messengership' (iqamat al-dalala 'ala thubut al-risala). He then states that the proof (dalil) is called a miracle (mu'jiza). The miracle is inherently divided into two types (qismayn), and it is necessary to discuss its basis (ma'khadh) alone, the explanation of its two types, and how it indicates the truthfulness of the person who brings it. Then under a new heading 'The source of the miracle' (ma'khadh al-mu'jiza), he explains that its source is 'ajz (incapacity), which is the opposite of qudra (power). The miracle is named mu'jiza because it manifests the incapacity of the one who is challenged (al-mutahadda) to produce something similar to it. Note that the promised division into two types is not detailed in this passage due to corruption (sact) at the end. The text ends abruptly after 'mu'ardataha' (opposing it). The passage establishes the essential connection between miracle and the concept of challenge (tahaddi) and the demonstration of human inability.

عجز
قدرة
تحدي
مأخذ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a miracle is defined simply as a supernatural act without connection to a challenge.
Correction: Al-Nasafi explicitly ties the miracle to a challenge (tahaddi) and demonstrates that its essence is showing the incapacity of the opponent to produce something similar.

Checkpoint
What is the etymological reason for calling a prophetic proof a 'mu'jiza' according to al-Nasafi?
A) Because it is a wonder that amazes people.
B) Because it shows the incapacity of the challenger to oppose it.
C) Because it comes from God's power alone.
D) Because it is a sign of divine pleasure.

Key Takeaway
A miracle is a proof of prophethood that demonstrates the challenger's incapacity to produce something similar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The miracle is rooted in human incapacity. Where in your life do you still act as if you are self-sufficient, ignoring your absolute need for God's guidance through the prophets?
?
If the proof of a true messenger is a challenge that no one can meet, what does that mean for your own response to the challenge of the Quran? Have you truly surrendered to its authority, or are you still trying to assert your own ability to judge it?
?
Al-Nasafi says the miracle shows the incapacity of the one challenged. When you face doubts about your faith, do you rely on your own reasoning, or do you humbly accept the signs God has sent? What would it take for you to fully admit your incapacity and accept the messenger?

The Claimant to Prophethood Must Provide Proof; Refutation of Ibadiyya
The author argues that a claimant to prophethood must present a miracle and refutes the Ibadiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a specific claimant to prophethood must produce a miracle as proof, and how this principle refutes the Ibadiyya sect.

Core Idea
A claimant's prophethood is contingent; only a proof can establish it.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi explains a foundational principle: while the existence of messengers as a category is necessary in God's knowledge, the specification of a particular individual as a messenger is contingent (mumkin). Therefore, every claimant must bring a proof to demonstrate his specific claim. The author then uses this principle to refute the Ibadiyya, who erroneously hold that a mere claim must be accepted without evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the technical terms like 'wajib al-wujud' and 'mumkinat'.
2
Understand the distinction between necessary and possible
The author distinguishes between the necessary existence of the messenger in general and the possible specification of this specific claimant. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
The proof for a necessary being is different from that for a contingent one.
3
Identify the requirement of proof
The passage states that because specification is among possibles, it requires a proof to establish it. See how the author deduces this logically.
The phrase 'فلا يثبت إلا بدليل يوجب ثبوته' is key.
4
Examine the refutation of the Ibadiyya
The author refutes the Ibadiyya who claim that a messenger's mere statement is obligatorily accepted without proof. Understand why their position is invalid according to the earlier principle.
The Ibadiyya are a sect of the Khawarij.
5
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider how this principle applies to other areas of theology: anything contingent requires evidence, and mere assertion is insufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو في نفسه واجب الوجود على ما قررنا ، فهو في حق كل فرد من أفراد الناس زمان جواز ورود الرسول وتعين هذا المدعي له في حيز الممكنات أيضا، إذ الدليل عند القائلين بوجوبه إن دل على ثبوته لا محالة، فلا دليل يدل على ثبوته في حق هذا الشخص المتعين، فلابد إذا لكل مدَّع يدعي ذلك في حق نفسه من إقامة دليل يدل على تعينه لذلك، لأن تعينه له من جملة الممكنات فلا يثبت إلا بدليل يوجب ثبوته. وبهذا يعرف بطلان قول الإباضية من الخوارج أن مجرد قول الرسول واجب القبول وأن تعرى عن الدليل، وأن الراد عليه يكفر في ساعته. إذ لا ثبوت لما جاز عليه العدم إلا بدليل يوجب ثبوته.
He is in Himself necessarily existent (wajib al-wujud) as we have established. So with respect to every individual among people, the time of the permissibility of the coming of a messenger and the specification of this claimant for it is also in the realm of possibles (mumkinat). For the proof (dalil), according to those who affirm its necessity, if it indicates his existence without fail, then no proof indicates his existence with respect to this specific person. Therefore, every claimant who claims this for himself must produce a proof that indicates his specification for it, because his specification for it is among the possibles, so it is not established except by a proof that necessitates its establishment. By this, the falsity of the statement of the Ibadiyya from the Khawarij is known: that the mere statement of the messenger is obligatorily accepted (wajib al-qubul) even if devoid of proof, and that the one who rejects it becomes an unbeliever instantly. For there is no establishment for that to which non-existence is possible except by a proof that necessitates its establishment.
Source pages p. 694

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. The author begins by stating that the existence of a messenger in general is necessary in God's knowledge, but the specification of a particular individual as a messenger is contingent (mumkin). Therefore, each claimant must bring a proof (miracle) to demonstrate his specific claim. The author then refutes the Ibadiyya sect of the Khawarij, who hold that a mere claim of prophethood must be accepted without proof, and that rejecting it leads to immediate disbelief. The author concludes that anything that can conceivably not exist (i.e., is contingent) cannot be established without a proof that necessitates its existence.

Key Terms
واجب الوجود
ممكنات
دليل
إباضية
خوارج
واجب القبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a prophet's claim alone suffices and must be accepted without evidence.
Correction: The author shows that the specification of a particular claimant is contingent and thus requires a proof to establish it.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that a specific claimant to prophethood must bring a proof?
A) Because prophethood is impossible without proof.
B) Because the existence of a specific messenger is contingent and needs a proof.
C) Because the Ibadiyya require it.
D) Because all messengers are necessarily known.

Key Takeaway
A contingent claim (like specific prophethood) requires a proof to be established; mere assertion is insufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, have you ever accepted a religious claim about God or prophethood without examining the proof for it? What does this lesson reveal about the danger of such blind acceptance?
?
The Ibadiyya thought rejecting a claimant without a miracle is disbelief. Is there anyone you have dismissed or judged harshly in religious matters without first seeking the evidence yourself? How can you change that?
?
If the proof for Muhammad's prophethood were to be questioned in your own heart, would you have a solid answer? What one concrete step will you take to strengthen your personal conviction in his prophethood based on proof?

Miracles as Divine Verification
Sanusi presents the concept of miracles as divine verification through analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a miracle is an act of God that directly verifies a prophet's claim, serving as His explicit affirmation.

Core Idea
A miracle is God's visible 'yes' to a prophet's message.

This lesson introduces the core concept that miracles are not mere wonders but divine acts of verification. Through clear analogies, the author illustrates how a unique act performed by an authority can authenticate a messenger. This foundation is essential for understanding the purpose and function of miracles in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the author's style of presenting a principle followed by analogies.
The language is dense, so take your time.
2
Examine the three analogies
Identify the three analogies: the secret letter, the ring, and the king's tree. Observe how each one demonstrates that an impossible act confirms the truth of a messenger.
Each analogy follows the same structure: a claimant, a sign known only to the authority, and the authority's response.
3
Understand the superiority over speech
Notice the author's point that a miracle is more reliable than spoken verification because speech can be ironic, but a miraculous act leaves no room for doubt or misinterpretation.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage concludes that a miracle, being an act exclusive to God, necessitates certain knowledge of divine affirmation and thus proves the prophet's truthfulness absolutely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدور جميع الخلق ولا قدرة عليه إلا الله تعالى، فإذا ادعى الرسالة، ثم قال آية صدق دعواي أن الله أرسلني أن يفعل الله كذا ففعل الله تعالى ذلك كان تصديقا من الله تعالى له فيما يدعي من الرسالة بما فعل من نقض المادة، فيكون ذلك كقوله عقيب دعواه هذه صدقت نظيره. وأن من أرسله غيره من البشر إلى آخر فقال الرسول للمرسل اكتب لي شيئًا من الأسرار التي كانت بينك وبينه فكتب له، وعلم المرسل إليه أن لا إطلاع على تلك الأسرار لغير المرسل كان ذلك دلالة صادقة على صدق مدعي الرسالة. وكذا لو قال رجل لصاحب الوديعة بين يدي المودع، إن كنت صادقا فيما أدعى من أمرك إياي بأخذ وديعتك منك فناولني خاتمك فناوله كان ذلك دليلاً على صدقه، كما لو قال صدقت بل أبلغ إذ القول به يحتمل الاستهزاء وهذا مما لا احتمال فيه بوجه من الوجوه، وكذا نظيره أن ملكا من الملوك لو كان في قصره شجرة عظيمة تقرر عند أهل مملكته وسكان حضرته أن لا قدرة لأحد من أهل قصر الملك على تحريك تلك الشجرة إلا الملك، ثم أن رجلاً من أهل القصر خرج إلى أهل البلدة وأخبرهم أنه رسول الملك إليهم وأن الملك يأمرهم بكذا وينهاهم عن كذا فكذبه أهل البلد في إدعاءه الرسالة من الملك إليهم، فنادى هذا الرجل بصوت يعلم أن الملك يسمعه لا محالة، أيها الملك أنك أرسلتني إلى رعيتك من أهل بلدتك وأمرتني أن أبلغ إليهم رسالة كذا بلغت إليهم فكذبوني في الرسالة، فإن كنت أرسلتني إليهم وكنت صادقًا فيما ادعيت من رسالتك إليهم فحرك الشجرة التي في قصرك ليكون تحركها آية صدقي فتحركت الشجرة بمرأى من أهل البلدة عقيب هذا النداء، كان ذلك دليلاً على صدق مقالة الرسول، وكان بمنزلة ما لو سمعوا من الملك أن قال للرسول: صدقت فيما ادعيت من رسالتي، أو قال لأهل البلدة: إنه صادق فيما بلغ إليكم من رسالتي، فكذا هذا. ثم ظهور الناقض للعادة عقيب دعوى الملك للرسالة، يوجب العلم يقينا أن الله تعالى هو الذي فعل ذلك إذ لا قدرة لغيره على مثل ذلك الفعل، ثم ذلك منه تصديق له فيدل ذلك على صدقه لا محالة، ثم ظهور مثله على يدي الكاذب في دعوى الرسالة ممتنع. واختلف المتكلمون في وجه امتناعه فقال من قال من أصحابنا باستدراك الحكمة والسفه والقبيح والحسن بمجرد العقول أن جهة امتناعه أنه لو ظهر ذلك على يد المتنبي، ولا دليل على صدق النبي إلا هذا لكان فيه التسوية بين الصادق والكاذب والحق والباطل، وسد طريق الوصول إلى الحق أصلاً وذا خارج من الحكمة، حتى إن ظهور ناقض العادة على يدي المتأله لما لم يكن سادًا طريق الوصول إلى الحق، ولا موجبًا التسوية بين الحق والباطل والمحق والمبطل لما في شخصه عن أمارات الحدوث، ودلائل كونه كاذبًا في دعواه كان ذلك ممكنا ممتنع بخلاف المتنبئ فإنه ليس في ذاته ما يدل على كذبه إذ هو من جوهر صادق في الدعوى لا فرق بينهما من حيث الذات فلا فرقان بينهما إلا من جهة المعجزة الناقضة للعادة فكان في ظهورها على يدي الكاذب ما مر من التسوية بين الحق والباطل وسد طريق الوصول إلى الحق
It is within the power of all creation and there is no power over it except Allah. So if he claims prophethood, then says: 'The sign of the truth of my claim that Allah sent me is that Allah does such and such,' and Allah does that, then that is an affirmation from Allah for him regarding what he claims of prophethood, by what He did of breaking the customary course. So that is like His saying after this claim: 'You have told the truth'—its analogue. And that if someone else among humans sent him to another, and the messenger said to the sender: 'Write for me something of the secrets that were between you and him,' and he wrote for him, and the one sent to knew that no one other than the sender has knowledge of those secrets, that would be a truthful indication of the truth of the claimant of prophethood. Likewise, if a man says to the depositary in the presence of the depositor: 'If you are truthful in what I claim regarding your order to me to take your deposit from you, then hand me your ring,' and he handed it over, that would be evidence of his truthfulness, as if he said 'you have told the truth'—rather it is more effective, because speech can be interpreted as mockery, while this has no possibility of that in any way. And similarly its analogue: that if a king had in his palace a great tree, established among the people of his kingdom and the inhabitants of his city that no one among the people of the king's palace has power to move that tree except the king, then a man from the people of the palace went out to the people of the town and informed them that he is the messenger of the king to them, and that the king commands them with such and such and forbids them from such and such, and the people of the town denied his claim of prophethood from the king to them. So this man called out in a voice that he knew the king would certainly hear: 'O king, you sent me to your subjects from the people of your town and commanded me to convey to them such and such a message, and I conveyed it to them, but they have denied me regarding the message. If you sent me to them and I am truthful in what I claimed of your message to them, then move the tree that is in your palace so that its movement becomes a sign of my truthfulness.' Then the tree moved in the sight of the people of the town after this call. That would be evidence of the truth of the messenger's statement, and it would be like if they heard the king say to the messenger: 'You have told the truth regarding what you claimed of my message,' or say to the people of the town: 'He is truthful in what he conveyed to you of my message.' So it is the same. Then the occurrence of a breaker of custom after a claim of prophethood necessarily causes certain knowledge that Allah did that, because no one else has power over such an act. Then that from Him is an affirmation for him, so that indicates his truth without doubt. Then the occurrence of something similar on the hand of a liar in claiming prophethood is impossible. The theologians differed regarding the aspect of its impossibility. Those of our companions who base the determination of wisdom, foolishness, evil, and good on reason alone said that the aspect of its impossibility is that if it occurred on the hand of a false prophet while there is no evidence for the truth of a prophet except this, then there would be a leveling between the truthful and the liar, truth and falsehood, and a complete closure of the path to reaching the truth, and that is outside of wisdom. Even though the occurrence of a breaker of custom on the hand of a pious person (mutā'allih) does not close the path to reaching the truth, nor necessitate a leveling between truth and falsehood and the truthful and the false, because in his person there are signs of temporality and evidence of his being a liar in his claim, so that is possible and not impossible, unlike the false prophet, for in his essence there is nothing that indicates his lie, since he is of the same substance as the truthful claimant; there is no difference between them in terms of essence, so there is no distinction except by virtue of the miracle that breaks custom. Therefore, in its occurrence on the hand of the liar, there is the aforementioned leveling between truth and falsehood and the closure of the path to reaching the truth.
Source pages p. 697

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument proving that miracles are divine verification for prophethood and explaining why they cannot occur on false prophets. The author begins by establishing that a miracle is an act within God's exclusive power. If a claimant to prophethood says, 'My sign is that God does such and such,' and God does it, that act—breaking the natural course—is equivalent to God saying 'You have told the truth.' The author provides three analogies: (1) A messenger asks the sender to write a secret only they know; when the recipient sees the secret written, it proves the messenger is truthful. (2) A man asks a depositary for a ring in the depositor's presence; handing it over proves his truthfulness, more reliably than speech because speech can be ironic. (3) A king's subject claims to be a messenger; the people disbelieve, so he calls on the king to move a unique tree that only the king can move. The king moves it, vindicating the messenger. The author then draws the conclusion: breaking normal laws after a claim to prophethood necessarily produces certain knowledge that God performed it, since no one else can do it, and this act serves as divine affirmation of the claim. He then states that such an act is impossible on the hand of a liar claiming prophethood. The theologians differed on why it is impossible. The author reports the view of his school (the Maturidis): those who ground moral categories like wisdom, foolishness, evil, and good in pure reason say that if a miracle appeared on a false prophet while the only proof for prophecy is miracles, that would equate truth and falsehood and block all access to truth—which is unwise. In contrast, a miracle on a pious person (mutā'allih) is possible without causing confusion because that person already shows signs of human limitation and other indicators of falsehood in his claim (e.g., his claim is contradicted by established prophecy). But a false prophet has no internal sign in his essence to distinguish him from a true prophet—both are human beings. Therefore, the only distinction is the miracle itself, so if it appeared on a liar, it would destroy the very purpose of distinguishing truth from falsehood.

Key Terms
ناقض العادة
تصديق
دعوى الرسالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because it is performed by God

C) Because it is louder
D) Because it happens faster

Key Takeaway
A miracle is God's direct act that verifies a prophet's claim, functioning as His explicit 'yes' without ambiguity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider the analogy of the king and the tree. If you were among the townspeople who saw the tree move, would you still doubt the messenger? What in your life causes you to doubt the clear signs of God?
?
The author says that a miracle is more effective than speech because speech can be interpreted as mockery. When you hear the Quran (which is a miracle itself), do you treat it as a direct affirmation from God, or do you still seek excuses not to obey? What would it take for you to respond as if God Himself said 'I sent him'?

Prophethood

Impossibility of Miracles on False Prophets
Sanusi explains why a miracle cannot occur on a false prophet, using rational argument from wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a miracle cannot appear on a false prophet, based on the need to preserve the distinction between truth and falsehood.

Core Idea
Allowing miracles on liars would destroy the very purpose of distinguishing truth from falsehood.

This lesson explores a critical corollary: if miracles are divine verification, they cannot occur on false prophets. The author presents the rational argument from the Maturidi school: if a miracle appeared on a liar while the only evidence for prophecy is the miracle itself, then truth and falsehood would become indistinguishable, which contradicts divine wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the second part of the source where the impossibility of miracles on false prophets is argued. Note the mention of differing opinions among theologians.
2
Identify the theological principle
The author states that a miracle on a false prophet would cause 'leveling' (taswiya) between truth and falsehood, blocking the path to truth. This is the core argument.
This is based on the Maturidi position that moral categories are known by reason.
3
Distinguish false prophet from pious person
Contrast the false prophet (mutanabbi) with the pious person (mutā'allih). The pious person already has signs of temporality or other indicators of falsehood, so a miracle on them does not cause confusion. But a false prophet has no such internal sign.
The difference lies in the claimant having other evidence of falsehood, not in the miracle itself.
4
Understand why it would be unwise
If miracles appeared on liars, there would be no way to know who is truthful, and the divine purpose of sending prophets would be defeated. Hence, it is impossible by divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدور جميع الخلق ولا قدرة عليه إلا الله تعالى، فإذا ادعى الرسالة، ثم قال آية صدق دعواي أن الله أرسلني أن يفعل الله كذا ففعل الله تعالى ذلك كان تصديقا من الله تعالى له فيما يدعي من الرسالة بما فعل من نقض المادة، فيكون ذلك كقوله عقيب دعواه هذه صدقت نظيره. وأن من أرسله غيره من البشر إلى آخر فقال الرسول للمرسل اكتب لي شيئًا من الأسرار التي كانت بينك وبينه فكتب له، وعلم المرسل إليه أن لا إطلاع على تلك الأسرار لغير المرسل كان ذلك دلالة صادقة على صدق مدعي الرسالة. وكذا لو قال رجل لصاحب الوديعة بين يدي المودع، إن كنت صادقا فيما أدعى من أمرك إياي بأخذ وديعتك منك فناولني خاتمك فناوله كان ذلك دليلاً على صدقه، كما لو قال صدقت بل أبلغ إذ القول به يحتمل الاستهزاء وهذا مما لا احتمال فيه بوجه من الوجوه، وكذا نظيره أن ملكا من الملوك لو كان في قصره شجرة عظيمة تقرر عند أهل مملكته وسكان حضرته أن لا قدرة لأحد من أهل قصر الملك على تحريك تلك الشجرة إلا الملك، ثم أن رجلاً من أهل القصر خرج إلى أهل البلدة وأخبرهم أنه رسول الملك إليهم وأن الملك يأمرهم بكذا وينهاهم عن كذا فكذبه أهل البلد في إدعاءه الرسالة من الملك إليهم، فنادى هذا الرجل بصوت يعلم أن الملك يسمعه لا محالة، أيها الملك أنك أرسلتني إلى رعيتك من أهل بلدتك وأمرتني أن أبلغ إليهم رسالة كذا بلغت إليهم فكذبوني في الرسالة، فإن كنت أرسلتني إليهم وكنت صادقًا فيما ادعيت من رسالتك إليهم فحرك الشجرة التي في قصرك ليكون تحركها آية صدقي فتحركت الشجرة بمرأى من أهل البلدة عقيب هذا النداء، كان ذلك دليلاً على صدق مقالة الرسول، وكان بمنزلة ما لو سمعوا من الملك أن قال للرسول: صدقت فيما ادعيت من رسالتي، أو قال لأهل البلدة: إنه صادق فيما بلغ إليكم من رسالتي، فكذا هذا. ثم ظهور الناقض للعادة عقيب دعوى الملك للرسالة، يوجب العلم يقينا أن الله تعالى هو الذي فعل ذلك إذ لا قدرة لغيره على مثل ذلك الفعل، ثم ذلك منه تصديق له فيدل ذلك على صدقه لا محالة، ثم ظهور مثله على يدي الكاذب في دعوى الرسالة ممتنع. واختلف المتكلمون في وجه امتناعه فقال من قال من أصحابنا باستدراك الحكمة والسفه والقبيح والحسن بمجرد العقول أن جهة امتناعه أنه لو ظهر ذلك على يد المتنبي، ولا دليل على صدق النبي إلا هذا لكان فيه التسوية بين الصادق والكاذب والحق والباطل، وسد طريق الوصول إلى الحق أصلاً وذا خارج من الحكمة، حتى إن ظهور ناقض العادة على يدي المتأله لما لم يكن سادًا طريق الوصول إلى الحق، ولا موجبًا التسوية بين الحق والباطل والمحق والمبطل لما في شخصه عن أمارات الحدوث، ودلائل It is within the power of all creation and there is no power over it except Allah. Commentary
This passage is a theological argument proving that miracles are divine verification for prophethood and explaining why they cannot occur on false prophets. The author begins by establishing that a miracle is an act within God's exclusive power. If a claimant to prophethood says, 'My sign is that God does such and such,' and God does it, that act—breaking the natural course—is equivalent to God saying 'You have told the truth.' (2) A man asks a depositary for a ring in the depositor's presence; handing it over proves his truthfulness, more reliably than speech because speech can be ironic. Why Miracles Cannot Occur on False Prophets
Al-Nasafi explains why miracles cannot occur on false prophets based on rational necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Māturīdī rational argument for why miracles cannot appear on false claimants to prophethood.

Core Idea
A miracle on a false prophet would make truth indistinguishable from falsehood.

Al-Nasafi presents a key Māturīdī argument: if miracles could support false claims, divine guidance would be impossible. The passage lays out the rational necessity that miracles must only confirm truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the claim that miracles on false prophets are impossible.
2
Identify the main premise
Identify the premise: a miracle after a claim to prophethood proves truthfulness because only God can do it.
3
Understand the reasoning
Follow the reasoning: if a miracle occurred on a false prophet, it would equate truth and falsehood, blocking the path to truth, which is contrary to divine wisdom.
4
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why would this be contrary to wisdom? How does this argument preserve the purpose of prophethood?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ظهور الناقض للعادة عقيب دعوى الملك للرسالة، يوجب العلم يقينا أن الله تعالى هو الذي فعل ذلك إذ لا قدرة لغيره على مثل ذلك الفعل، ثم ذلك منه تصديق له فيدل ذلك على صدقه لا محالة، ثم ظهور مثله على يدي الكاذب في دعوى الرسالة ممتنع. واختلف المتكلمون في وجه امتناعه فقال من قال من أصحابنا باستدراك الحكمة والسفه والقبيح والحسن بمجرد العقول أن جهة امتناعه أنه لو ظهر ذلك على يد المتنبي، ولا دليل على صدق النبي إلا هذا لكان فيه التسوية بين الصادق والكاذب والحق والباطل، وسد طريق الوصول إلى الحق أصلاً وذا خارج من الحكمة، حتى إن ظهور ناقض العادة على يدي المتأله لما لم يكن سادًا طريق الوصول إلى الحق، ولا موجبًا التسوية بين الحق والباطل والمحق والمبطل لما في شخصه عن أمارات الحدوث، ودلائل كونه كاذبًا في دعواه كان ذلك ممكنا ممتنع بخلاف المتنبئ فإنه ليس في ذاته ما يدل على كذبه إذ هو من جوهر صادق في الدعوى لا فرق بينهما من حيث الذات فلا فرقان بينهما إلا من جهة المعجزة الناقضة للعادة فكان في ظهورها على يدي الكاذب ما مر من التسوية بين الحق والباطل وسد طريق الوصول إلى الحق
Then the occurrence of a miracle (nāqiḍ al-ʿādah) following a claim of prophethood necessitates certain knowledge that Allah Almighty is the one who did that, for no other has power over such an act. Then that from Him is a confirmation of him, so it indicates his truthfulness without doubt. Then the occurrence of the like of it on the hand of a liar in the claim of prophethood is impossible. The mutakallimūn differed concerning the aspect of its impossibility. Those of our companions who deduce wisdom (ḥikmah) and foolishness (safah) and evil (qabīḥ) and good (ḥasan) purely by reason said that the aspect of its impossibility is that if that were to appear on the hand of a false prophet (mutanabbī), and there is no proof of the prophet's truthfulness except this, then it would involve equating the truthful and the liar, truth and falsehood, and blocking the path to reaching truth entirely, and that is outside of wisdom. So much so that the occurrence of a miracle on the hand of a pious devotee (mutaʾallih), since it does not block the path to reaching truth nor necessitate equating truth and falsehood and the truthful and the false, due to there being in his person signs of temporal origination (amārāt al-ḥudūth) and proofs of his being a liar in his claim, that was possible, not [by] impossibility, unlike the false prophet, for there is nothing in his essence that indicates his falsehood, since he is of the same substance as a truthful claimant, no difference between them in terms of essence. So there is no distinction between them except by means of the miraculous sign (muʿjiza) that breaks custom. So in its occurrence on the hand of the liar would be what was mentioned of equating truth and falsehood and blocking the path to reaching truth.
Source pages p. 698

Commentary
This passage presents the Māturīdī argument for the impossibility of miracles (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) appearing on the hands of false claimants to prophethood. The author begins by restating the general principle: when a miracle occurs after a claim to prophethood, it proves the claimant's truthfulness because only God can perform it, and it serves as His confirmation. Then he asserts that such a miracle cannot occur on a liar. He then presents the Māturīdī reasoning: if miracles could appear on false prophets, and if the only proof of a prophet's truthfulness is the miracle itself (since there is no intrinsic difference between a truthful and a false claimant in terms of essence), then it would equate truth and falsehood and block the path to knowing the truth. This would be contrary to divine wisdom (ḥikmah). The author contrasts this with the case of a pious devotee (mutaʾallih) who performs marvels (karāmāt). Such marvels are possible because the devotee has other signs (like temporal origination) that indicate he is not a prophet, so no confusion arises. But the false prophet has no such intrinsic sign, so a miracle would cause confusion.

Key Terms
ناقض العادة
التسوية بين الحق والباطل
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles could be given to false prophets to test people's faith.
Correction: The Māturīdīs argue that this would defeat the purpose of guiding to truth, as it would make truth and falsehood indistinguishable.

Checkpoint
According to the Māturīdī argument, why is it impossible for a miracle to appear on a false prophet?
A) Because God would be unable to create a miracle for a liar.
B) Because it would equate truth and falsehood and block the path to truth, contrary to divine wisdom.
C) Because miracles are inherently connected to truth and cannot exist with falsehood by definition.
D) Because false prophets are not worthy of such power.

Key Takeaway
Miracles cannot occur on false prophets because that would destroy the very purpose of prophethood: clear guidance to truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate religious claims today, do you rely on clear rational evidence or do you follow desires and habits? How does this lesson challenge you to prioritize truth over comfort?
?
Al-Nasafi says that if God allowed miracles on false prophets, the path to truth would be blocked. In your life, have you ever ignored clear evidence because it was inconvenient? What would it mean for you to commit to seeking truth even when it is difficult?
?
The Māturīdīs argue that God's wisdom necessitates distinguishing truth from falsehood. How does your own pursuit of truth reflect trust in God's wisdom? Are you willing to let go of falsehoods you hold dear?

Distinction Between Prophets and Saints Regarding Miracles
Al-Nasafi distinguishes the possibility of miracles for saints from the impossibility for false prophets.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why miracles can appear on saints but not on false prophets, according to the Māturīdīs.

Core Idea
Saints have intrinsic signs of non-prophethood, so their marvels do not cause confusion.

Al-Nasafi contrasts the case of a false prophet with a pious devotee (muta'allih). While a miracle on a false prophet would be confusing, a marvel on a saint is permissible because the saint's temporal limitations (amārāt al-ḥudūth) already indicate he is not a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the comparison between the false prophet and the saint.
2
Identify the distinguishing factor
Notice that the saint has signs of temporal origination (amārāt al-ḥudūth) that show he is not a prophet, while the false prophet has no such intrinsic sign.
3
Understand why the saint's case is different
The saint's marvels do not block the path to truth because his humanity and other signs prevent confusion. The false prophet lacks any such sign.
4
Apply the principle
Explain why miracles (muʿjizāt) are exclusive to prophets but marvels (karāmāt) can occur for saints.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ظهور الناقض للعادة عقيب دعوى الملك للرسالة، يوجب العلم يقينا أن الله تعالى هو الذي فعل ذلك إذ لا قدرة لغيره على مثل ذلك الفعل، ثم ذلك منه تصديق له فيدل ذلك على صدقه لا محالة، ثم ظهور مثله على يدي الكاذب في دعوى الرسالة ممتنع. واختلف المتكلمون في وجه امتناعه فقال من قال من أصحابنا باستدراك الحكمة والسفه والقبيح والحسن بمجرد العقول أن جهة امتناعه أنه لو ظهر ذلك على يد المتنبي، ولا دليل على صدق النبي إلا هذا لكان فيه التسوية بين الصادق والكاذب والحق والباطل، وسد طريق الوصول إلى الحق أصلاً وذا خارج من الحكمة، حتى إن ظهور ناقض العادة على يدي المتأله لما لم يكن سادًا طريق الوصول إلى الحق، ولا موجبًا التسوية بين الحق والباطل والمحق والمبطل لما في شخصه عن أمارات الحدوث، ودلائل كونه كاذبًا في دعواه كان ذلك ممكنا ممتنع بخلاف المتنبئ فإنه ليس في ذاته ما يدل على كذبه إذ هو من جوهر صادق في الدعوى لا فرق بينهما من حيث الذات فلا فرقان بينهما إلا من جهة المعجزة الناقضة للعادة فكان في ظهورها على يدي الكاذب ما مر من التسوية بين الحق والباطل وسد طريق الوصول إلى الحق
Then the occurrence of a miracle (nāqiḍ al-ʿādah) following a claim of prophethood necessitates certain knowledge that Allah Almighty is the one who did that, for no other has power over such an act. Then that from Him is a confirmation of him, so it indicates his truthfulness without doubt. Then the occurrence of the like of it on the hand of a liar in the claim of prophethood is impossible. The mutakallimūn differed concerning the aspect of its impossibility. Those of our companions who deduce wisdom (ḥikmah) and foolishness (safah) and evil (qabīḥ) and good (ḥasan) purely by reason said that the aspect of its impossibility is that if that were to appear on the hand of a false prophet (mutanabbī), and there is no proof of the prophet's truthfulness except this, then it would involve equating the truthful and the liar, truth and falsehood, and blocking the path to reaching truth entirely, and that is outside of wisdom. So much so that the occurrence of a miracle on the hand of a pious devotee (mutaʾallih), since it does not block the path to reaching truth nor necessitate equating truth and falsehood and the truthful and the false, due to there being in his person signs of temporal origination (amārāt al-ḥudūth) and proofs of his being a liar in his claim, that was possible, not [by] impossibility, unlike the false prophet, for there is nothing in his essence that indicates his falsehood, since he is of the same substance as a truthful claimant, no difference between them in terms of essence. So there is no distinction between them except by means of the miraculous sign (muʿjiza) that breaks custom. So in its occurrence on the hand of the liar would be what was mentioned of equating truth and falsehood and blocking the path to reaching truth.
Source pages p. 698

Commentary
This passage presents the Māturīdī argument for the impossibility of miracles (khawāriq al-ʿādāt) appearing on the hands of false claimants to prophethood. The author begins by restating the general principle: when a miracle occurs after a claim to prophethood, it proves the claimant's truthfulness because only God can perform it, and it serves as His confirmation. Then he asserts that such a miracle cannot occur on a liar. He then presents the Māturīdī reasoning: if miracles could appear on false prophets, and if the only proof of a prophet's truthfulness is the miracle itself (since there is no intrinsic difference between a truthful and a false claimant in terms of essence), then it would equate truth and falsehood and block the path to knowing the truth. This would be contrary to divine wisdom (ḥikmah). The author contrasts this with the case of a pious devotee (mutaʾallih) who performs marvels (karāmāt). Such marvels are possible because the devotee has other signs (like temporal origination) that indicate he is not a prophet, so no confusion arises. But the false prophet has no such intrinsic sign, so a miracle would cause confusion.

Key Terms
المتأله
أمارات الحدوث
التسوية بين الحق والباطل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saints' marvels (karāmāt) are the same as prophets' miracles and could also be used to claim prophethood.
Correction: Saints' marvels are distinct because saints have other signs showing they are not prophets; they do not claim prophethood, so no confusion arises.

Checkpoint
Why is it possible for a miracle to appear on a pious devotee (muta'allih) but not on a false prophet?
A) Because the devotee is more righteous than the false prophet.
B) Because the devotee has signs of temporal origination that distinguish him from a prophet.
C) Because the false prophet's claim is inherently false and God never supports falsehood.
D) Because miracles only appear on prophets by definition.

Key Takeaway
Marvels can occur on saints because they have intrinsic signs that they are not prophets, preserving the clarity of divine guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who appears spiritually powerful or performs extraordinary deeds, do you automatically assume they are a guide from God? How does al-Nasafi's argument challenge you to look for the proper signs before trusting such claims? What would it cost you to be wrong?
?
The passage says a false prophet is indistinguishable in essence from a true prophet—only the miracle (mu'jiza) sets them apart. Where in your life have you relied on a person's charisma, reputation, or outward piety to judge their truthfulness? What does this passage demand you change about how you discern religious authority?
?
You are not a prophet, but you carry the 'signs of temporal origination'—your mortality, weakness, and neediness. How often do you ignore these signs and act as if you have ultimate authority or knowledge? What would it mean for you to humbly accept your creatureliness in your daily interactions and decisions?

Ashʿarī Argument: Miracles Impossible for False Claimants
Al-Nasafi presents the Ashʿarī argument that miracles on false claimants imply God's incapability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī rationale for why miracles cannot appear on false claimants to prophethood, and how this differs from the case of pious individuals.

Core Idea
Miracles on false prophets would make God incapable of distinguishing truth from falsehood — and that is impossible.

The Ashʿarī theologians developed a sophisticated argument to explain why miracles cannot occur on false prophets. It hinges not on any inherent property of falsehood, but on the logical implications such an event would have for God's ability to guide humanity. Al-Nasafi presents this reasoning, and then distinguishes it from the case of pious devotees who are not claiming prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the repetition in the Arabic—likely a scribal duplication.
The repetition may indicate emphasis or a copyist error.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the statement that a miracle on a false prophet would necessitate God's incapability (taʿjīz). Understand why this is the central argument.
3
Understand the concept of taʿjīz
Taʿjīz (incapability) is impossible for God. The argument moves from the hypothetical (miracle on false prophet) to the impossible (taʿjīz), concluding the hypothetical must also be impossible.
This is a standard kalam reductio ad absurdum.
4
Examine the exception for the pious devotee
Notice that the argument does not apply to the mutaʾallih (pious devotee) because the devotee does not claim prophethood, so no confusion arises.
5
Reflect on the principle 'la safah'
The passage begins with the Ashʿarī principle that there is no foolishness except what is prohibited by revelation. Consider how this relates to the argument's logical structure.
This distinguishes the Ashʿarī approach from those who use rational good and evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهب جمهور الأشعرية ومن قال من متكلمي أهل الحديث : إنه لا سفه إلا ما ورد عنه النهي، إلى أن جهة امتناع ظهور الناقض للعادة على يدي مدعي الرسالة كاذبًا ، أن ظهوره على يديه يوجب تعجيز الباري جلا وعلا عن إقامة الدلائل على صدق الصادق في دعوى الرسالة، وإثبات التفرقة بين الحق والباطل، وتعجيز الباري جلا وعلا عن إقامة الدلائل على صدق الصادق في دعوى الرسالة، وإثبات التفرقة بين الحق والباطل، وتعجيز الباري جل وعلا محال، حتى إن إظهار ذلك على يدي المتأله لما لم يكن موجبًا تعجيزه جل وعلا عن ذلك لما في شخص المدعي ما يدل على كذبه لم يكن ذلك ممتنعا
And the majority of the Ashʿarīs and those who said from among the mutakallimūn of ahl al-ḥadīth – that there is no foolishness except what is prohibited by revelation – [held] that the aspect of the impossibility of the occurrence of a miracle (nāqiḍ al-ʿādah) on the hand of a false claimant to prophethood is that its occurrence on his hand necessitates the incapability (taʿjīz) of the Exalted and Glorious Creator from establishing proofs of the truthfulness of the truthful one in his claim of prophethood and establishing the distinction between truth and falsehood. And the incapability of the Exalted and Glorious Creator is impossible. So much so that the occurrence of that on the hand of a pious devotee (mutaʾallih), since it does not necessitate His incapability (glorified and exalted be He) concerning that, because in the person of the claimant there is something that indicates his falsehood, it is not impossible.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage presents the Ashʿarī argument for the impossibility of miracles on false prophets. The author states that the majority of the Ashʿarīs and some Ḥadīth theologians who hold that 'foolishness' is only what revelation forbids (i.e., they reject rational good and evil) argue that the reason miracles cannot occur on false claimants is that doing so would imply that God is incapable (taʿjīz) of establishing clear proofs of the truthful prophet's truthfulness and of distinguishing truth from falsehood. Since incapability is impossible for God, it is impossible for a miracle to appear on a false claimant. The author then explains that this does not apply to the pious devotee (mutaʾallih), because the devotee already has indications of falsehood (such as not claiming prophethood), so God is not rendered incapable.

Key Terms
تعجيز الباري
لا سفه إلا ما ورد عنه النهي
ناقض العادة
المتأله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ashʿarī argument is about God being unable to create miracles for liars.
Correction: Actually, the argument is about the logical implication that such miracles would erase the distinction between truth and falsehood, which would imply God's incapability (taʿjīz) – an impossibility.

Checkpoint
What is the key reason the Ashʿarīs give for the impossibility of miracles on false prophets?
A) It would violate the principle that miracles must be morally good.
B) It would imply that God is incapable of providing a clear proof of truth, which is impossible.
C) It would lead to a conflict with human reason.
D) It would cause confusion among believers.

Key Takeaway
The Ashʿarīs argue that miracles on false prophets are impossible because they would entail God's incapability, but miracles on pious non-prophets are not impossible because they do not confuse the distinction between truth and falsehood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a claim about religion that seems impressive or 'miraculous', do you actively check it against the clear distinctions God has provided in revelation, or do you let the spectacle convince you? What would it mean to truly trust God's wisdom in distinguishing truth from falsehood?
?
The Ash'aris argue that God would never allow a false prophet to perform a miracle because it would erase the line between truth and falsehood. In your own life, where do you struggle to see that line? Is there any 'miracle' you are holding onto that might actually be a sign of your own desire rather than God's truth?
?
God's incapability is impossible—yet we often live as if God is incapable of guiding us, relying on our own emotions or cultural norms over His clear proofs. What is one area where you need to submit to God's clear distinction instead of your own assumptions, starting today?

The Ḥadīth Theologian’s Argument on Miracles
Ḥadīth theologian's argument on miracles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ḥadīth theologian’s argument that miracles are inherently proofs of truthfulness, and why they cannot occur on false prophets.

Core Idea
Miracles are inherently proofs of truthfulness, just as perfect acts are proofs of knowledge.

Al-Nasafi introduces a third position on why miracles cannot accompany false prophethood. The Ḥadīth theologians argue that the very essence of a miracle makes it an intrinsic sign of truthfulness, analogous to how a perfectly crafted act is an inseparable sign of knowledge. This passage presents their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the analogy with 'al-fiʿl al-muḥkam'.
2
Understand the analogy
The Ḥadīth theologian compares the miracle-truthfulness connection to the perfect act-knowledge connection. Both are essential and inseparable.
3
Distinguish from other views
Recognize that this argument is different from the Māturīdī wisdom-based argument. This one relies on essential connection.
The author will later endorse the Māturīdī view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال بعض متكلمي أهل الحديث : أن جهة امتناع ذلك أن المعجزة دلالة الصدق لعينها، فلا يجوز وجودها مع غير الصدق كالفعل المحكم دلالة العلم لعينه فلم يجز اقترانه بالجهل فكذا هذا. والمحققون منهم على الوجه الأول، والله الموفق. وإذا علم هذا فبعد ذلك يحتاج إلى التكلم في موضعين: أحدهما في أن الرسالة قد ثبت في الجملة لا محالة فيما مضى من الأزمنة. والثاني : في إثبات رسالة بعض الرسل على طريق التعيين. ونتكلم في الفصلين جميعًا بما يوجب صحة ما ندعي في كل فصل – إن شاء الله تعالى
And some of the mutakallimūn of ahl al-ḥadīth said: The aspect of its impossibility is that the miracle (muʿjiza) is a proof of truthfulness in its very essence, so it is not permissible for it to exist with something other than truthfulness, just as a perfectly crafted act is a proof of knowledge in its very essence, so it is not permissible for it to be coupled with ignorance. So likewise this. And the verifiers among them are upon the first view. And God is the grantor of success. And when this is known, then after that there is need to speak on two points: the first, that prophethood is established in general without doubt in past times; and the second, in establishing the prophethood of some of the messengers by way of specification. And we will speak in both sections regarding what necessitates the correctness of what we claim in each section – if God wills.
Source pages p. 699

Commentary
This passage contains three elements. First, the author presents a third argument from some Ḥadīth theologians: miracles are inherently (li-ʿaynihā) proofs of truthfulness, just as a well-made act inherently indicates knowledge. Therefore, they cannot exist with falsehood. This is a direct essential connection. Second, the author states that the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn) among the mutakallimūn (i.e., the respected scholars) adopt the first view – that is, the Māturīdī position based on wisdom (ḥikmah). He concludes with 'and God is the grantor of success.' Third, he signals a transition: now that this matter is settled, he needs to discuss two subsequent topics: proving that prophethood existed in general in past times, and then proving the specific prophethood of individual messengers. He promises to address both.

Key Terms
المعجزة دلالة الصدق لعينها
الفعل المحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ḥadīth theologian's argument is identical to the Māturīdī view.
Correction: The Ḥadīth theologian argues from essential connection; the Māturīdī view argues from divine wisdom.

Checkpoint
According to the Ḥadīth theologian, what does a perfectly crafted act (الفعل المحكم) indicate?
A) Truthfulness
B) Knowledge
C) Power
D) Wisdom

Key Takeaway
The Ḥadīth theologian argues that miracles are intrinsically linked to truthfulness, making false miracles logically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'لا يجوز وجودها مع غير الصدق' (it is not permissible for it to exist with anything other than truthfulness), consider: Are there areas of your life where you are trying to use something that is inherently a sign of truth to cover up falsehood? For example, using religious language or actions to hide insincerity? How will you align your outward signs with inner truthfulness starting today?
?
The analogy with the perfectly crafted act shows that a perfect act cannot come from ignorance. Think of your own acts of worship or daily deeds: Are any of them 'perfectly crafted' outwardly but lacking true knowledge or sincerity? What step will you take to ensure your deeds are coupled with genuine knowledge and truthfulness?
?
The Ḥadīth theologian insists on an essential, inseparable link between miracle and truthfulness. Reflect: In your own relationship with Allah, is there any sign or blessing you have that you are not attributing to its true source? Could it be that you are tarnishing a clear sign by associating it with falsehood or ingratitude? Commit to examining your heart tonight.

The Author’s Conclusion and Upcoming Topics
Author's endorsement and roadmap

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the author endorses the Māturīdī view and outlines the next two topics: general proof of prophethood and specific identification of messengers.

Core Idea
The verifiers adopt the first view; now we must prove prophethood in general and in particular.

After presenting several arguments on miracles, al-Nasafi states that the verifiers (al-muḥaqqiqūn) among the theologians adopt the first view—the Māturīdī position based on wisdom. He then signals a shift to the next major subject: establishing the reality of prophethood in general, and then the specific prophethood of individual messengers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation to see the author's final stance and roadmap.
Pay attention to the phrase 'al-muḥaqqiqūn ʿalā al-wajh al-awwal'.
2
Identify the endorsement
The author declares that the verifiers are upon the first view – the Māturīdī wisdom-based argument.
This resolves the earlier debate.
3
Note the transition
The author says 'when this is known' and then lists two topics to discuss next.
The structure shows the logical progression of the book.
4
Understand the two topics
First topic: general proof that prophethood existed. Second topic: specific proof that certain individuals were prophets.
This sets up the coming chapters.

What would it take for you to submit to their judgment even when it goes against your inclination?
?
The author declares that after settling this matter, there is need to speak on two topics: general prophethood and specific prophets. This shows a structured, cumulative approach to learning. Think of your own understanding of Islam: have you built your beliefs on a solid foundation, step by step, or have you skipped to conclusions without the groundwork? Repent and commit to learning with order.
?
The author ends with 'if God wills' (insha'Allah) acknowledging that completion depends on divine will. When you plan your studies or your life, do you genuinely feel that your efforts are nothing without God's permission, or do you proceed as if you are in control? Pause and make du'a for God's success in your journey.

Proof of Prophethood: The Argument from Specialized Knowledge
Nasafi argues that knowledge of substances cannot be acquired by reason or experience, proving divine origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that specialized knowledge about substances (foods, poisons, medicines) must come from divine teaching, establishing prophethood.

Core Idea
Some knowledge cannot be attained by reason or experience; it requires a prophet.

Imam al-Nasafi presents a powerful argument for the necessity of prophethood. He observes that the world contains substances that are essential for human survival but that cannot be distinguished by our senses or intellect alone. Yet people have learned to use them. This lesson unpacks this argument, showing how it leads to the conclusion that such knowledge comes from a divinely taught source—a prophet.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text aloud or silently. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the argument is structured: observation, problem, resolution.
2
Identify the key observation
Notice that the author begins with the division of substances into foods, poisons, and medicines. He states that distinguishing them is impossible by experience (tajribah) alone.
This sets up the need for a teacher.
3
Trace the chain of teaching
The argument shows that people learn from a teacher, and that teacher from another, until the chain ends with one who knows directly from the Creator. This chain proves prophethood.
4
Reflect on the logical necessity
Ask yourself: why is it impossible for this knowledge to be discovered by ordinary human faculties? The author claims it's because even the most intelligent person cannot know without a teacher.
This is the core of the proof.
